# Node.js: Microservices

> In this fast-paced era of distributed systems, mastering microservices—not just deploying services, but truly understanding the patterns and principles that drive them— is essential for developers. And in organizations large and small, Node.js is often the platform of choice for building microservices architectures. In this course, Daniel Khan
shows you how to use Node.js to create a microservice 

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072) | 3h 39m | Advanced | 15K learners
> [Jump to Path Context ↓](#path-context)

- [[Daniel Khan]]

#### What you should know
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-you-should-know?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=0)** - This course is not for beginners.
>
> **[0:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=2)** If you're new to Express, start with my courses "[[Building a Website with Node.js and Express.js]]," and "Advanced Express," which you can find in the LinkedIn library.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=11)** These courses will provide you with all the fundamentals you need.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=15)** To get the most out of this course, make sure you know JavaScript including variables, functions and callbacks.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=20)** You'll also need to be familiar with the ES6 syntax, like classes, error functions, spread syntax, and objective structuring since they'll popup throughout the videos.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=30)** Plus, understanding promises and async await is important.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=36)** When it comes to Node.js, you should know how to install modules using npm and how to start an application.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=42)** You also need a good handle on Express and how HTTP works.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=46)** It will be super helpful if you have a basic understanding of REST and REST APIs.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=51)** You can find these topics covered in my courses that I mentioned earlier, but there are also dedicated courses for specific topics in the library.

>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/getting-the-exercise-files-from-github?u=76281980&t=56)** It has to end on blank .git, and then I hit Return.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/getting-the-exercise-files-from-github?u=76281980&t=61)** Next I type git config --bool core.bare false.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/getting-the-exercise-files-from-github?u=76281980&t=74)** If this all looks rather akin to you, don't worry for me as well, but this is how it's done.

>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/getting-the-exercise-files-from-github?u=76281980&t=88)** and then I type git branch.

> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/getting-the-exercise-files-from-github?u=76281980&t=93)** Now you see it's a lot of branches are coming down and that's it.

> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/getting-the-exercise-files-from-github?u=76281980&t=98)** Now we have downloaded all the branches of all the exercise files.

#### Understanding the sample apps code
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/understanding-the-sample-apps-code?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/getting-insights-with-opentelemetry-and-jaeger?u=76281980&t=57)** It helps gather timing data needed to troubleshoot latency problems in service-oriented architectures.

#### Setting your mission
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/setting-your-mission?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/setting-your-mission?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We know that we want to turn our monolithic shop application into a set of microservices, but before we get technical and get started, why would we want to do this?
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/setting-your-mission?u=76281980&t=11)** Let's first agree on some reasons why we even putting all the work into it.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/setting-your-mission?u=76281980&t=16)** A lot has been written already about microservices and splitting up monolith, and you'll find the whole course by me on the topic in the library.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/setting-your-mission?u=76281980&t=24)** Please be aware that microservices aren't the silver bullet and while they have benefits, they can easily also turn into a nightmare if not used wisely.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/setting-your-mission?u=76281980&t=33)** That said, I want to lead into this course with two simple goals.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/setting-your-mission?u=76281980&t=38)** First goal is I want to increase scalability.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/setting-your-mission?u=76281980&t=42)** Monolithic applications can be challenging to scale.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/setting-your-mission?u=76281980&t=46)** We aim to transform application into microservices to make it easier to scale different components independently based on their respective needs.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/setting-your-mission?u=76281980&t=55)** This means for me that we want to build our system in such a way that multiple instances of one service can exist at the same time to allow load balancing between them.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/setting-your-mission?u=76281980&t=68)** My second goal is I want to make it easier to maintenance services.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/setting-your-mission?u=76281980&t=72)** In a microservice architecture, services can be maintained independently and also by different teams.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/setting-your-mission?u=76281980&t=79)** It's even possible to deploy different versions at the same time if the microservice system is built in such a way.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/setting-your-mission?u=76281980&t=87)** The goal here is we want to ensure that services can be deployed independently and even in different versions.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/setting-your-mission?u=76281980&t=95)** So we need some versioning and some version management here to enable that.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/setting-your-mission?u=76281980&t=101)** With that, I think we are ready to really now delve into the code and we will start with that in the next chapter.

> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter?u=76281980&t=9)** We have an HTML Front End.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter?u=76281980&t=11)** These are the rendered views we see in the browser.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter?u=76281980&t=14)** Then we have a set of routes.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter?u=76281980&t=16)** These routes, at this point, represent the different URLs a user can access like /shop.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter?u=76281980&t=23)** I didn't write out all the routes here and just use this box here as a placeholder.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter?u=76281980&t=29)** Below that, we have the business logic.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter?u=76281980&t=32)** This logic is today in so-called service classes for catalog, order, user, and cart.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter?u=76281980&t=38)** We saw them before when we looked through the application.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter?u=76281980&t=41)** The service classes for catalog, order, and user use MongoDB as its backend to store the data and cart uses Redis.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter?u=76281980&t=52)** And also for the sake of completeness, I will omit this from now on.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter?u=76281980&t=55)** The front end also uses Redis as a session store, but we won't touch this functionality.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter?u=76281980&t=63)** Back to the service classes, they make it rather easy for us to find a good way to split out functionality as there is pretty much a one-to-one mapping between the service class and what will later be a standalone microservice.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter?u=76281980&t=76)** In real life applications, it can be more complicated to find the boundaries within the business logic.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter?u=76281980&t=83)** The goal is now to create one service that can replace a piece of business logic from the main application, and that shows the catalog that handles all catalog related operations.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter?u=76281980&t=94)** It provides access to all the items in the shop and also lets us edit and delete them.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter?u=76281980&t=101)** The catalog service itself is also nothing else but an express application.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter?u=76281980&t=106)** It also comes with a set of routes and some service class that talks to the database.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter?u=76281980&t=111)** These routes represent a so-called REST API.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter?u=76281980&t=114)** This is the HTTP interface that other applications can use to talk to our service.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter?u=76281980&t=120)** As REST APIs are so important and often misunderstood, we will spend a full video to quickly cover the fundamentals while we design the REST API for our first service.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter?u=76281980&t=131)** But before we do that, let's quickly create the express application that will become our catalog microservice.

#### Designing a REST API for the catalog service
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=0)** - REST APIs are a key concept we'll use to design our microservice architecture.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=5)** REST stands for representational state transfer which is an architectural style for designing network applications.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=15)** A Rest API uses HTTP to perform operations known as CRUD operations, create, read, update and delete on data.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=26)** The idea is to use standard HTTP methods to manipulate resources which are identified using URLs.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=34)** It's stateless, meaning each HTTP request should contain all the information needed to perform the operation.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=43)** This makes REST APIs very scalable and they're widely adopted in software development today.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=50)** As mentioned before, the two important building blocks of any REST API are so-called HTTP verbs or methods and URLs.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=60)** Let's look at an URL first.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=62)** We use them every day in the browser.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=64)** It consist of a protocol, here, HTTP, a domain name and some path to a resource.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=72)** So what are HTTP verbs now?
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=75)** When you simply access a website in the browser, the verb for it is GET so the HTTP method is GET, and we also call this a GET request.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=85)** If you ever created HTML form, you for sure also know a POST request with the verb POST.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=92)** It differs from a GET request as it can contain a body with a pedal, like for instance, some form data.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=99)** This is where the usually used verbs end but there are more.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=102)** The ones also relevant to REST are PUT, which can, like POST, carry a payload and there is DELETE.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=109)** What REST not does is to give these verbs a semantical meaning relevant to APIs.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=117)** As such, GET is used to request data from a resource, POST is used to create a new resource, PUT is used to update the resource, and DELETE is used to delete the resource.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=132)** So now what does this mean for our catalog API?
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=135)** Let's take a look at the respective service class we have now in our shop application.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=140)** For that, I head over into Visual Studio Code.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=143)** And there, I open shopper, server, services, and then we have the catalog service.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=151)** And if we scroll through it, you see that we have a getAll method for getting all items.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=157)** We have a getOne method for getting one item by item ID.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=163)** We have a create method to create a new item.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=167)** And we also a update method to update an item with new data.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=172)** And then there is also a remove method to remove or delete an item.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=179)** This gives us a very clear path to how our REST API for the catalog service should look like.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=185)** And I've sketched it out here.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=187)** So GET/items should return all items.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=190)** GET/items/:itemID should return an item by its ID.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=194)** POST/items should create a new item with the payload in the request body.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=200)** PUT/items/itemID should update an item identified by its ID using the data in the payload.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=207)** And DELETE/items/itemID should delete an item identified by its ID.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=212)** By structuring our APIs in a restful way, we can have a clear standardized interface for our services that other services can interact with.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=220)** Let me say that we just scratched the surface of REST.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=223)** In fact, it was introduced by Roy Fielding in his PhD thesis.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=228)** For our purposes though, this should be enough.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/designing-a-rest-api-for-the-catalog-service?u=76281980&t=231)** Enough theory for now, let's build these routes or API endpoints in the next video.

### 4. Creating a Service Registry

#### What's your goal for this chapter?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter-22664578?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter-22664578?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Host] By now we have two applications, the shopper frontend and the catalog service.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter-22664578?u=76281980&t=5)** We know that the catalog service listens on a random part and it can even run on a completely different host or in some container or even in the cloud.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter-22664578?u=76281980&t=14)** The problem is now that the shopper application cannot know where to find the catalog service, neither its IP Address nor its port is known.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter-22664578?u=76281980&t=24)** We would need something like a phone book that contains a list of services and the locations and the so-called service registry is exactly that.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter-22664578?u=76281980&t=33)** We would need something like a phone book that contains a list of all services, and their locations and the so-called service registry is exactly that.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter-22664578?u=76281980&t=43)** Let's see how this works.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter-22664578?u=76281980&t=45)** The service registry is nothing but a service itself.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter-22664578?u=76281980&t=48)** It just listens on a known port.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter-22664578?u=76281980&t=51)** There are other ways to make even this dynamic but from my experience, abstraction needs to end somewhere where things just get too complicated.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter-22664578?u=76281980&t=59)** Now, when a service starts up, it does a rest call to the service registry and registers itself with its name, its IP and its port.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter-22664578?u=76281980&t=68)** The service registry now stores this information.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter-22664578?u=76281980&t=72)** Next, when the shop app now wants to call the catalog service it first asks the service registry for its location.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter-22664578?u=76281980&t=80)** The service registry ideally now returns the location of the catalog service.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter-22664578?u=76281980&t=86)** Now, when the shopper app wants to call the catalog service it'll first ask the service registry for its location.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter-22664578?u=76281980&t=94)** The service registry hopefully then returns this location and with that information we can finally make the call to the catalog service.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter-22664578?u=76281980&t=104)** This is the bare minimum functionality.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter-22664578?u=76281980&t=107)** In this chapter, we will also make sure that services can unregister themselves on shutdown and also will be removed if this suddenly disappear without notice.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter-22664578?u=76281980&t=114)** This is called a timeout.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter-22664578?u=76281980&t=116)** So are you ready?
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter-22664578?u=76281980&t=117)** Let's get started.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

> **CLI Commands:** make (3), find (1)
> **Code Keywords:** let (2)
> **Definitions:** is called (1)
> **Speakers:** - [host] (1)

#### Removing expired services
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/removing-expired-services?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/removing-expired-services?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Later services will register and unregister themselves, but there may be cases when a service just shuts down non-gracefully with no chance to unregister.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/removing-expired-services?u=76281980&t=10)** Let's make it a rule that services need to send some kind of a heartbeat to reregister themselves continuously, and that the service that we haven't seen for some amount of time is considered to be expired.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/removing-expired-services?u=76281980&t=25)** We already have everything to build this inner registry, services can update themselves and there is already a timeout property.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/removing-expired-services?u=76281980&t=32)** So all that is left is to add one function that does the work.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/removing-expired-services?u=76281980&t=36)** So I go into lib registry and there right on top, I will add a new method, cleanup.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/removing-expired-services?u=76281980&t=48)** So the first thing we will do is we get the current term const now = Math.floor(new Date() ) and we divided by a thousand.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/removing-expired-services?u=76281980&t=64)** This gives us the Unix timestamp in seconds.
>

#### Adding heartbeat and unregistering on shutdown
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/adding-heartbeat-and-unregistering-on-shutdown?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript

>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/adding-heartbeat-and-unregistering-on-shutdown?u=76281980&t=14)** For that, JavaScript has something very handy for us.

> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/adding-heartbeat-and-unregistering-on-shutdown?u=76281980&t=29)** So this will run some function in a defined interval and we want to run register every, let's say, 10 seconds because we know that the registry will clean up after 15 seconds.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/adding-heartbeat-and-unregistering-on-shutdown?u=76281980&t=45)** So we should be safe with that.

#### Service monitoring with OpenTelemetry and Jaeger
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/service-monitoring-with-opentelemetry-and-jaeger?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/service-monitoring-with-opentelemetry-and-jaeger?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We have now working service registry and also two services that are used by our main application.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/service-monitoring-with-opentelemetry-and-jaeger?u=76281980&t=6)** And now it's a good time to look into monitoring with OpenTelemetry in Jaeger.

### 6. Authenticating APIs

#### API authentication with JWT
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In this chapter, we will look at how we can make API endpoints more secure by adding authentication.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=6)** This is an incredibly important and often forgotten topic.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=10)** Let's see how we handle our user authentication today.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=15)** For some operations, we require a locked in user like for creating or editing items.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=20)** Here we see the login route in our shopper app but now we haven't moved authentication into a separate service, but this isn't important at this point.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=29)** In line 10, we see that when a user logs in the form we try to authenticate the user, and if this succeeds, we store the user at the in the session.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=38)** From then on, we trust the user, but we also revalidate it on every request that happens up here in lib middlewares.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=48)** There we have this code block starting in line 13 that will first check if there is a user ID on the session, and then try to load it from the database.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=59)** So if a user is deleted or their privileges change, this will also immediately be applied.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=65)** Here's the whole flow in a more schematic way.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=68)** Again, it doesn't matter if the user service is within the monolith or not.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=73)** When the user authenticates, we trust that this is a valid user and if we, for instance, call the catalog service to create an item, the service needs to trust that the user has successfully logged in.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=85)** And this is pretty unsecure.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=87)** In fact, if you put the catalog service out on the internet today, anyone could create a modify items as all the trust is on the caller.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=97)** There has to be a better way to secure endpoints and there is, it's called JSON Web Token, in short, JWT.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=105)** At its core, JWT is a token that can carry a payload which can be read by anyone but only created and validated using a secret.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=114)** It's the de facto standard for establishing trust between services.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=119)** So let's look at the same flow with JWT.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=124)** We again send username and password using the form.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=127)** The server will again try to authenticate the user and if this works, we'll now create and return this JWT token.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=136)** Along with other properties, this token includes a payload, which contains in our case, user information, including their id.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=145)** Next, the front end will persist this token in the session as before.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=149)** But now with every request to the service, we send the token in a header called authentication and a property called bearer.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=157)** On the service, we will then decrypt and maybe also validate the token signature.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=164)** JWT tokens are not a solution to all problems and they come with limitations as well.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=168)** Let's take a quick look.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=170)** If someone eavesdrops the token, the request can still be forged.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=174)** Means that you definitely lead a secure connection where the token is transmitted.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=179)** - Anyone can decrypt JWT without the secret.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=183)** So decrypting and looking at the payload does not require you to have the secret.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=188)** It's just for validating.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=191)** On the plus side, without the secret, an attacker cannot create and sign a token to assume another user's identity.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=199)** We will use the token as a standardized means to sign and transport the user data.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=205)** JWT is more complex than what I covered here.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=208)** So please look at [jwt.io](https://jwt.io) to learn more on this topic.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/api-authentication-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=213)** In this chapter, we will quickly create the user service before we migrate our current system to understand and use JWT authentication.

#### Add JWT tokens to the user service
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/add-jwt-tokens-to-the-user-service?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript

>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/add-jwt-tokens-to-the-user-service?u=76281980&t=7)** For that, I open first my user service.

> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/add-jwt-tokens-to-the-user-service?u=76281980&t=9)** So I head into workspace, microservices, and user service, and there, I open routes index js.

> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/add-jwt-tokens-to-the-user-service?u=76281980&t=16)** You also see on the terminal that I have the user service also here open, and I will now quit nodemon with Control C.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/add-jwt-tokens-to-the-user-service?u=76281980&t=26)** And then I will first install a module that gives us JSON web token.
>

> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/protecting-endpoints-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=185)** So let's fix this back.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/protecting-endpoints-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=187)** I remove the wrong token and I update this here.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/protecting-endpoints-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=193)** Let's click on Manage Items again.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/protecting-endpoints-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=195)** And now let's try to delete this item.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/protecting-endpoints-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=198)** And you see now it's really deleted.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/protecting-endpoints-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=200)** So we now have successfully protected our catalog routes that do the CRUD operations.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/protecting-endpoints-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=207)** Of course, we can now extend it to other operations, for instance, for the order management so that you can only set an order as shipped when this token is present and the user is an admin.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/protecting-endpoints-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=223)** I leave this to you, it's very similar to what we did just now.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/protecting-endpoints-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=227)** There's one more corner case I want to quickly cover.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/protecting-endpoints-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=230)** This is when you go into Manage Users, you see that I can change the user and remove the admin status.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/protecting-endpoints-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=237)** I could also delete this user.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/protecting-endpoints-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=239)** And this is all not accounted for now in the logic we have because we don't look into the database anymore when the user comes along, we really trust the token.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/protecting-endpoints-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=249)** And there are different means how to handle that.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/protecting-endpoints-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=252)** For instance, creating a blocked list with tokens somewhere that is also consulted to check if a token is invalidated.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/protecting-endpoints-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=260)** JWT and endpoint security would be worth a course on its own.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/protecting-endpoints-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=265)** So what I want you to take away from this chapter is that sensitive endpoints need to be protected, and JWT is a good way to do it.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/protecting-endpoints-with-jwt?u=76281980&t=274)** If you ever want to apply JWT in your own applications, please have a close look at [jwt.io](https://jwt.io) to really understand the whole topic end to end.

### 7. Adding Fault Tolerance and Resilience

#### What's your goal for this chapter?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter-22663546?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter-22663546?u=76281980&t=9)** Let's have a look at our architecture, and I use Jaeger for that.

>

>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter-22663546?u=76281980&t=64)** Resilience helps the system to continue to function despite individual service failures.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter-22663546?u=76281980&t=71)** So what does resilience really mean?
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter-22663546?u=76281980&t=74)** We need to gracefully react to errors and also recover from failures.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter-22663546?u=76281980&t=78)** The goal is not to avoid failures, as such, but we want to respond to them effectively.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/what-s-your-goal-for-this-chapter-22663546?u=76281980&t=84)** And in the end, the end goal is to prevent downtime or any data loss.
>

#### Adding caching to reduce load and bridge outages
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/adding-caching-to-reduce-load-and-bridge-outages?u=76281980)

>

> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/adding-caching-to-reduce-load-and-bridge-outages?u=76281980&t=75)** So allItemsCache = result.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/adding-caching-to-reduce-load-and-bridge-outages?u=76281980&t=79)** And now if there is an error, we simply return allItemsCache.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/adding-caching-to-reduce-load-and-bridge-outages?u=76281980&t=87)** So let's try this out.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/adding-caching-to-reduce-load-and-bridge-outages?u=76281980&t=88)** So what I will first do, I will just reload the page to populate the cache and next I will now stop our catalog-service.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/adding-caching-to-reduce-load-and-bridge-outages?u=76281980&t=99)** So I hit Ctrl + C.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/adding-caching-to-reduce-load-and-bridge-outages?u=76281980&t=102)** And let's now try to reload the page.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/adding-caching-to-reduce-load-and-bridge-outages?u=76281980&t=108)** And you see it still works.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/adding-caching-to-reduce-load-and-bridge-outages?u=76281980&t=109)** If I go into Manage Items, even this still works.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/adding-caching-to-reduce-load-and-bridge-outages?u=76281980&t=113)** Likely when I go into Edit item, yeah, there you see there nothing shows up.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/adding-caching-to-reduce-load-and-bridge-outages?u=76281980&t=119)** And we would need to handle this problem now in some other graceful way maybe restrict maybe the item management or set the whole page into a degraded state and let the user know that something is wrong.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/adding-caching-to-reduce-load-and-bridge-outages?u=76281980&t=134)** But at least the page as such is not completely down.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/adding-caching-to-reduce-load-and-bridge-outages?u=76281980&t=138)** This has different implications.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/adding-caching-to-reduce-load-and-bridge-outages?u=76281980&t=139)** For instance, search engines, Google, for instance, will of course penalize you if the page is down and no items are shown maybe a whole weekend, yeah.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/adding-caching-to-reduce-load-and-bridge-outages?u=76281980&t=150)** So with this measure, you are at least in the degraded but at least somewhat functional state.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/adding-caching-to-reduce-load-and-bridge-outages?u=76281980&t=156)** And as you saw, it's a very easy fix.

#### Using queues for decoupling
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/using-queues-for-decoupling?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/using-queues-for-decoupling?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One goal in microservice architectures is to avoid tight coupling.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/using-queues-for-decoupling?u=76281980&t=5)** It's hard to decouple actions that are needed to show data on the website instantly, but when it comes to posting data to a service in a file-and-forget way, doing that asynchronously through some intermediate service removes any dependency between caller and receiving service.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/using-queues-for-decoupling?u=76281980&t=23)** Queues are a common way to accomplish that, and the basic principle is very simple.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/using-queues-for-decoupling?u=76281980&t=29)** Basically, they provide a way to publish messages to it.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/using-queues-for-decoupling?u=76281980&t=32)** In our shopper app, we store orders.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/using-queues-for-decoupling?u=76281980&t=35)** Right now, the order functionality is still part of the monolith, but we will change that.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/using-queues-for-decoupling?u=76281980&t=40)** When a customer clicks on Buy, there are several actions performed, and eventually an order is created.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/using-queues-for-decoupling?u=76281980&t=46)** This is typically a file-and-forget call.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/using-queues-for-decoupling?u=76281980&t=48)** I don't need any feedback by the order-service at this point.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/using-queues-for-decoupling?u=76281980&t=52)** If you use a queue, these Create Order messages are stored in this queue regardless of the status of the order-service.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/using-queues-for-decoupling?u=76281980&t=60)** When the order service is available, it consumes this queue, and after it consumes a message, it can acknowledge that it received it successfully.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/using-queues-for-decoupling?u=76281980&t=69)** In this case, this message will then be removed from the queue.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/using-queues-for-decoupling?u=76281980&t=74)** There are several queuing systems available, and if you're on an infrastructure provider like AWS Azure or Google Cloud, they will come out of the box.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/using-queues-for-decoupling?u=76281980&t=82)** In this course, we will be using RabbitMQ to publish and consume order messages asynchronously.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/using-queues-for-decoupling?u=76281980&t=89)** RabbitMQ is available for all common operating systems and has a client library for Node.js.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/using-queues-for-decoupling?u=76281980&t=94)** As such, it's a good candidate for showing the basic functionality of queues in this course.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/node-js-microservices-22685072/using-queues-for-decoupling?u=76281980&t=99)** In the next video, I will cover how to set it up.
